      U NDERSTANDING L EARNING WITH S LICED -WASSERSTEIN
002   R EQUIRES R E - THINKING I NFORMATIVE S LICES

005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review

009                                               A BSTRACT
010
011            The practical applications of Wasserstein distances (WDs) are constrained by their
012            sample and computational complexities. Sliced-Wasserstein distances (SWDs)
013            provide a workaround by projecting distributions onto one-dimensional subspaces,
014            leveraging the more efficient, closed-form WDs for one-dimensional distributions.
015            However, in high dimensions, most random projections become uninformative due
016            to the concentration of measure phenomenon. Although several SWD variants
017
               have been proposed to focus on informative slices, they often introduce additional
               complexity, numerical instability, and compromise desirable theoretical (metric)
018
               properties of SWD. Amidst the growing literature that focuses on directly modi-
019
               fying the slicing distribution, which often face challenges, we revisit the classic
020            Sliced-Wasserstein and propose instead to rescale the 1D Wasserstein to make all
021            slices equally informative. Importantly, we show that with an appropriate notion
022            of slice informativeness, rescaling for all individual slices simplifies to a single
023            global scaling factor on the SWD. This, in turn, translates to the standard learning
024            rate search for gradient-based learning in common ML workflows. We perform ex-
025            tensive experiments across various machine learning tasks showing that the classic
026            SWD, when properly configured, can often match or surpass the performance of
027            more complex variants. We then answer the following question:
028                      Is Sliced-Wasserstein all you need for common learning tasks?
029
030
031
      1   I NTRODUCTION
032   Data representation in machine learning involves encoding the unique characteristics of individual
033   data points and capturing the relationships between them, with a focus on optimizing performance
034   for specific downstream tasks. Optimal transport (OT) theory (Villani et al., 2009; Peyré et al., 2019)
035
      compares data distributions by finding an optimal transportation plan that minimizes the expected
      cost of moving mass between them, leading to the popular Wasserstein distance (WD) central to
036
      many learning applications (Khamis et al., 2024). However, the computational complexity of OT
037
      solvers poses a significant bottleneck when calculating the WD. In cases of discrete measures or
038   sample-based scenarios, which are common in machine learning, the problem typically reduces
039   to linear programming with time complexity O(N 3 log N ), space complexity O(N 2 ), and sample
                         1
040   complexity O(N − d ), where N is the number of support points and d the data dimensionality. These
041   unfavorable scaling properties, particularly the curse of dimensionality in sample complexity, make
042   WD impractical for many real-world applications. To address these challenges, several approaches
043   have been proposed, including entropic regularized OT (Cuturi, 2013), smooth OT (Blondel et al.,
044   2018; Manole et al., 2024)), and sliced OT (Bonneel et al., 2015).
045
      The Sliced-Wasserstein distances (SWD), (Rabin et al., 2012; Bonneel et al., 2015) project high-
046   dimensional distributions onto 1D subspaces and aggregate the closed-form OT solutions in these
047   subspaces. This method is particularly attractive because 1D Wasserstein distances can be computed
048   efficiently with a time complexity of O(N log N ) and a space complexity of O(N ) for discrete
049   measures. Additionally, SWD provides a metric between probability distributions that retains many
050   desirable properties of the Wasserstein distance (WD), such as being statistically and topologically
051   equivalent to WD, while being more computationally tractable (Nadjahi et al., 2020). Notably,
                                           1
052   with a sample complexity of O(N − 2 ), SWD avoids the curse of dimensionality. However, a key
053   drawback of SWD is its projection complexity, which requires exponentially more slices as the data
      dimensionality increases.


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      The projection complexity of SWD has motivated several lines of work that aim to enhance the
055   effectiveness of the slicing approach, especially in addressing variance reduction (Nguyen & Ho,
056   2023), approximation error reduction (Nguyen et al., 2023), and slicing complexity (Kolouri et al.,
057   2019; Deshpande et al., 2019; Nguyen et al., 2020; 2024a; Nguyen & Ho, 2024; Nguyen et al., 2024b).
058   This is particularly relevant in high-dimensional machine learning settings where data often has
059   supports in low-dimensional subspaces. These SW variants are data-driven, focusing on identifying
060   the most informative slices for capturing distributional differences in the data. For instance, Max-SW
061   (Deshpande et al., 2019) and DSW (Nguyen et al., 2020) seek to find slices/projections that maximize
062   the differences between the data distributions. GSW (Kolouri et al., 2019) and ASW (Chen et al.,
063
      2020) extend SW by allowing ‘non-linear’ projections to capture complex data structures. EBSW
      (Nguyen & Ho, 2024) designs an energy-based slicing distribution that is parameter-free and has
064
      the density proportional to an energy function of the projected 1D distance. MSW (Nguyen et al.,
065
      2024a) imposes a first-order Markov structure to avoid redundant, independent projections. More
066   recently, RPSW (Nguyen et al., 2024b) proposes using the normalized differences between random
067   samples from the two distributions to ensure that the projections are sampled from the subspace
068   in which the data resides. These methods improve the performance of SW in various downstream
069   tasks and have significantly expanded the tools at disposal for both researchers and practitioners
070   alike. Nonetheless, the elegant extensions also come with increased computational cost, numerical
071   instability, complicated design choices, and often losing the metricity of the SWD.
072   In this paper, we argue that the standard SW, with proper hyperparameters, can often match or surpass
073   the performance of more complex variants in many learning tasks while retaining its simplicity and
074   theoretical guarantees. Our key insight is that when d-dimensional data have k-dimensional supports,
075   where k ≪ d, almost all random slices θ ∼ U(Sd−1 ) can be decomposed into an informative
                                                                                             ⊥
076   component θD ∈ Rk within the data subspace and its orthogonal complement θD              ∈ Rd−k . This
077   implies most slices still carry relevant information for distinguishing distributions, proportional
078   to ∥θD ∥. By appropriately scaling the distance per slice, we get better gradient for learning. In
079   expectation, we show that, with our defined notion of informativeness, scaling for all slices (based
080
      on their informativeness) simplifies to scaling the SWD by a single scalar factor. In gradient-based
      learning, this means finding an appropriate learning rate is equivalent to getting informative slices for
081
      free. This allows the classical SWD to adapt to the data’s intrinsic dimensionality without explicitly
082
      limiting the computation to the subspace. We provide theoretical justification and empirical evidence,
083   offering a fresh perspective on SW, particularly in high-dimensional settings.
084
085   By revisiting the celebrated SW with these insights, we aim to elucidate the performance gap between
086
      the original formulation and recent variants in the existing literature. We emphasize that our work
      does not diminish the valuable contributions of these variants, which have greatly advanced our
087
      understanding of Sliced-Wasserstein. Rather, we offer a complementary perspective that highlights
088
      the potential of the standard SW when properly integrated into learning tasks. Along that line, we
089   remark that the related line of specialized methods that respects the data geometry (Rabin et al., 2011;
090   Bonet et al., 2022; Martin et al., 2023; Quellmalz et al., 2023; Bonet et al., 2024; Tran et al., 2024)
091   remains valuable when the manifold constraint on the data is readily known.
092
      In common ML settings where data is supported, or nearly supported, on a k-dimensional subspace
093
      embedded in a d-dimensional space, our specific contributions can be summarized as follows:
094
095          • We introduce the ϕ-weighting formulation unifying various SW variants. In this framework,
096            we propose reweighing all one-dimensional Wasserstein distances based on slice informa-
097            tiveness instead of directly modifying the slicing distribution, as commonly done in the
098            literature. We show that with an appropriate notion of slice informativeness, in expectation,
099
               this leads to an equivalence between the SWD in the ambient space and the data effective
               subspace. (See 20).
100
             • Our findings translate to scaling the classic SW by a single global constant to get better
101
               learning gradients. We show that this reduces solving the problem of non-informative slices
102            to the learning rate search for the classic SW, a process that is already a standard in ML
103            workflows. In other words, we get informative slices for free with the classic SW.
104          • We perform a comprehensive learning rate sweep across a wide range of experiments,
105            including gradient flow (on 3 classic toy datasets, MNIST images, CelebA images), color
106            transfer (3 sets of images), deep generative modeling on the FFHQ dataset (unconditional
107            generation and unpaired translation with SW). We show that the classic SW, with appropriate
               hyperpameters, perform competitively with more advanced methods in these settings.

108
      Notations. We let Rd denote a d-dimensional inner product space, and we denote the unit hypersphere
109   in this space by Sd−1 = {θ ∈ Rd : ∥θ∥2 = 1}. Additionally, we denote by P(Rd ) the set of
110   probability measures on Rd endowed with the σ-algebra of Borel sets, and by Pp (Rd ) ⊂ P(Rd ) the
111   subset of those measures with finite p-th moments. For a measurable function f : Rd → R defined
112   by f (x) = θ⊤ x such that θ ∈ Sd−1 , we denote the pushforward of a measure µ ∈ P(Rd ) through f
113   as f# µ. Particularly, θ# µ is the pushforward measure of µ under the projection x 7→ θ⊤ x.
114
115   2   BACKGROUND ON S LICED -WASSERSTEIN
116   Let µ ∈ Pp (Rd ) and ν ∈ Pp (Rd ) be two probability measures of interest.
117
118
      The Wasserstein distance (WD). The p-WD between µ and ν is:
                                               Z

                            Wpp (µ, ν) = inf          ∥x − y∥pp dπ(x, y),                                (1)
120                                            π∈Π(µ,ν)   Rd ×Rd
121
      with Π(µ, ν) = {π ∈ Pp (Rd × Rd ) : π(A × Rd ) = µ(A), π(Rd × A) = ν(A)} for all measurable
122
      sets A ⊂ Rd . In one dimension (d = 1), the p-WD admits the following closed-form solution:
123                                            Z 1
124
                                  Wpp (µ, ν) =     |Fµ−1 (z) − Fν−1 (z)|p dz,                     (2)
125                                                0
126   where Fµ , Fν are the cumulative distribution functions (CDF) of µ and ν, respectively. For empirical
127   measures, 2 becomes a Monte Carlo sum that can be calculated by averaging the dp (·, ·) between
128   sorted samples. In general, this translates to a highly favorable time complexity of O(N log N ) and
129   gives rise to the following Sliced-Wasserstein distance.

      Sliced-Wasserstein (SW). The SW distance between µ and ν is defined as:

144   large compared to d, which is undesirable since the time complexity of SW scales linearly with L.
145
146   3   OTHER R ELATED W ORK
147   Subspace-constrained Optimal Transport. Recent works propose computing optimal transport
148   (OT) in lower-dimensional subspaces (Paty & Cuturi, 2019; Bonet et al., 2021b; Muzellec & Cuturi,
149   2019) to improve both efficiency and robustness for high-dimensional data. 1) Subspace Detours
150   (Bonet et al., 2021b; Muzellec & Cuturi, 2019) constrain transport plans to be optimal when projected
151   onto a chosen subspace. This enables efficient extension of low-dimensional transport solutions to
152   the full space. 2) Subspace Robust Wasserstein (Paty & Cuturi, 2019) considers the worst-case
153   transport cost over all possible low-dimensional projections. Interestingly, this can be computed by
154
      minimizing the sum of the k largest eigenvalues of theR transport plan second-order moment matrix
                                Pk
155   Sk2 (µ, ν) = minπ∈Π(µ,ν) l=1 λl (Vπ ) where Vπ := (x − y)(x − y)T dπ(x, y) is the second-order
156   displacement matrix for a coupling π, and λl (Vπ ) is its l-th largest eigenvalue.
157   Gaussian Sliced-Wasserstein. Earlier works ((Sudakov, 1978; Diaconis & Freedman, 1984; Reeves,
158   2017)) establish several central limit theorems showing that under mild conditions, low-dimensional
159   projections of high-dimensional data converge to Gaussians. Nadjahi et al. (2021) leverages this
160   concentration of measure phenomenon and shows the Gaussian SW distance is equivalent to the
161   classical SWD: SWpp (µ, ν; N (0, d1 Id )) = Cd,p SWpp (µ, ν; U(Sd−1 )), where Cd,p is a dimensionality-
      dependent constant. They then propose an efficient approximation of the SWD without simulation.


                                                          3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      4    R EVISITING SLICED WASSERSTEIN D ISTANCES : A S UBSPACE P ERSPECTIVE
163
164   The main challenge. Many machine learning problems involve high-                               𝜃 ~𝒰(𝕊𝑑−1 )
165   dimensional data that has a low-dimensional structure. Formally, this
166
      phenomenon, known as the manifold hypothesis, states that for a dataset
      X ⊂ Rd , there exists a k-dimensional manifold M where k ≪ d such ℝ𝑘                                𝜶𝑈𝑇 𝜃
167
      that X approximately lies on M (Fefferman et al., 2016). For instance,                      𝑇
                                                                                                 𝑈 𝜃
168
      rigorous dimensionality estimation methods applied to common datasets
169   like MS-COCO (Lin et al., 2014) and ImageNet (Deng et al., 2009) sug-
170   gest k < 50 (Pope et al., 2021), despite their ambient dimension d being
171   orders of magnitude larger. While these manifolds are generally non- Figure 1: Rescaling the
172   linear, they admit local linear approximations via their tangent spaces. 1D Wasserstein based on
173   Moreover, in practice, data features typically have strong linear correla- slice informativeness.
174   tions, allowing techniques like Principal Component Analysis (PCA) to
175   identify a principal subspace that captures most of the data variance. This subspace approximation is
176   particularly relevant in the context of Sliced-Wasserstein distance (SWD). It is known from Kolouri
177
      et al. (2019) that when slices θ are sampled uniformly from Sd−1 , the probability that a random slice
      is nearly orthogonal to any fixed direction increases exponentially with dimension. Specifically, for a
178
      unit vector x0 representing a principal direction in the data subspace:
179                                                             2
180                              Pr (|⟨θ, x0 ⟩| ≤ ϵ) > 1 − e−dϵ ,    θ ∼ U(Sd−1 ).                          (5)
181   This concentration of measure phenomenon implies that as dimensionality d grows, most random
182   slices become nearly orthogonal to the principal directions of the data subspace. Consequently, the
183   corresponding 1D Wasserstein distances contribute minimally to the SWD. This effect, which we
184   refer to as the slice non-informativeness, limits the effectiveness of SWD in high-dimensional spaces.
185
      Current approaches: Designing the slicing distribution. Sampling-based methods seek to define a
186   non-uniform slicing distribution that focuses on discriminative directions. Optimization-free methods
187   (Nguyen & Ho, 2024; Nguyen et al., 2024b) are objectively faster but do not yield true metrics. Other
188   methods (Nguyen et al., 2020; 2024a) yield proper metrics but are more computationally expensive
189   due to the optimization involved. In the limit, the Max variants use discrete slicing distributions that
190   require global optimality to be metrics, which is generally intractable in practice. Empirically, without
191   careful hyperparameter tuning, the different variants face numerical instability in the larger learning
192   rate regimes, likely because of the overemphasizing on directions with large projected distances.
193   A novel perspective: Rescaling 1D Wasserstein distances. These challenges in directly redefining
194   the slicing distribution motivates us to take a second look at the conventional wisdom of sampling
195   informative slices. We propose an alternative formulation that reweights each 1D Wasserstein based
196   on the informativeness of the corresponding slice/projecting direction (See Figure 1 for illustration).
197   By defining the notion of an informative slice based on its alignment with the effective data subspace,
198   we demonstrate that it is possible to reweight for all slices by a global constant on the SWD.
199   This maintains the efficiency and theoretical properties of the classical Sliced-Wasserstein. The
200
      implications of this finding for using SWD in gradient-based learning will be discussed subsequently.
      To formalize this approach, we introduce the following assumption and definitions:
201
202   Assumption 4.1 (Effective Subspace Structure). Let µd , ν d ∈ P (Rd ) be probability measures. We
203   say (µd , ν d ) has k-dimensional effective structure if:
204
            1. There exists a semi-orthogonal matrix U ∈ Rd×k (i.e., U T U = Ik ) such that
205
206                                     supp(µd ), supp(ν d ) ⊂ Vk := col-span(U ).
207                                                                               ′

208
            2. k is minimal, meaning that there does not exist any U ′ ∈ Rd×k with k ′ < k s.t (1) holds.
209   We refer to Vk as the effective subspace (ES) of µd , ν d , and k as their effective dimensionality (ED).
210
      Note: In the Appendix A.9, we also discuss results when this assumption does not hold.
211
212
      Definition 4.2 (Informative slices). Let ϕ : Sd−1 → R+ be a function that assigns importance values
      to projection directions θ ∈ Sd−1 based on their relevance in comparing data distributions. Different
213
      approaches compute this importance in various ways. For instance, Max-SW (Deshpande et al.,
214
      2019), Markovian SW Nguyen et al. (2024a), and EBSW (Nguyen & Ho, 2024) implicitly use
215
                                            ϕµ,ν (θ) = Wpp (θ# µ, θ# ν),                                    (6)


                                                          4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      to measure the informativeness of θ. On the other hand, RPSW (Nguyen et al., 2024b) implicitly uses
217
218                        ϕµ,ν (θ; µ, ν, γκ ) = E(X,Y )∼µ×ν [γκ (θ; PSd−1 (X − Y ))],                   (7)
219
220   where γκ is a location-scale distribution (e.g., vMF) and PSd−1 is the projection onto Sd−1 .
221
      One may also refer to ϕµ,ν as the discriminant function. However, we define informativeness more
222   broadly, allowing for a broader set of assumptions about data structure. Other ways to quantify
223   informativeness may be appropriate depending on the context where prior information on the data is
224   available. We refer to further related details in Remark 4.6.
225
226
      Defining ϕ in terms of 1D Wassersteins may not always be desirable. It requires calculating them to
      find out how informative the slices are, even when the calculations are not always used in computing
227
      the final distances (Nguyen et al., 2024a). Furthermore, defining ϕ based on the input measures µ, ν
228
      could introduce complex dependencies that make the triangle inequality difficult to prove (Nguyen &
229   Ho, 2024; Nguyen et al., 2024b). Motivated by 4.1, we propose a principled notion of informativeness
230   that avoids both issues and leads to a significantly simplified solution.
231
      Definition 4.3 (ES-aligned informative slices). Given Vk = span(U ), where U ∈ Rd×k is an
232
      orthogonal matrix, we define the ES-aligned informative function ϕU : Sd−1 → [0, 1] as:
233
234
                                               ϕU (θ) = ∥U ⊤ θ∥.                                         (8)
235
236   Intuitively speaking, ϕU corresponds to how much information θ contains about the data if it is
237   projected into the space spanned by U . Higher ϕU (θ) is considered more (ES-aligned) informative.
238   Remark 4.4. ϕU (θ) has the following basic properties: a) 0 ≤ φU (θ) ≤ 1 for all θ ∈ Sd−1 , b)
239   φU (θ) = 1 iff θ ∈ span(U ) ∩ Sd−1 and φU (θ) = 0 iff θ ⊥ span(U ), and c) For any orthogonal
240   matrix Q ∈ Rk×k , φU (θ) = φU Q (θ).
241

      4.2 M ISALIGNED RANDOM PROJECTIONS ARE IMPLICITLY DOWNWEIGHED BY A SCALAR
271
      Under Assumption 4.1, we will show that the 1D Wasserstein corresponding to each random projection
272
      is weighted by a scalar related to the (ES-aligned) informativeness of that projection.
273
274   The case for 1D effective subspaces. Let V1 = span(u) where u ∈ Sd−1 , and suppose
275   supp(µd ), supp(ν d ) ⊂ V1 . Given θ ∈ Sd−1 , we can decompose it uniquely as θ = θV1 + θV1⊥ ,
276   where θV1 = (u⊤ θ)u and θV1⊥ ⊥ V1 . For any x ∈ V1 , we have x = (x⊤ u)u, and θ⊤ x can thus be
277   decomposed as:
278                            θ⊤ x = (θV1 + θV1⊥ )⊤ x = θV⊤1 x = (u⊤ θ)⊤ (u⊤ x).               (11)
279
280   This implies that for any slice θ, the projected distributions θ# µd and θ# ν d are equivalent (up to
281   scaling) to the distributions obtained by projecting µd and ν d onto u. Specifically:
282
                                     Wpp (θ# µd , θ# ν d ) = |u⊤ θ|p Wpp (u# µd , u# ν d ).                            (12)
283
284   Generalizing to higher-dimensional effective subspaces. We extend the idea from one dimension to
285   a k-dimensional subspace Vk and investigate how the reweighting function ρϕ (ϕU (θ)) = ∥U ⊤ θ∥−p
286   adjusts the contributions of slices in higher dimensions.

307   Rescaling to equalize informativeness. Assumption 4.1 gives rise to the fact that each one-

339    Since σk (θk ) integrates to 1 over Sk−1 , and Wpp θ#   k k k k
                                                                 µ , θ# ν is integrated over all θk , we can
340   express the right-hand side as Cd,k · SWpp (µk , ν k ; σk ). Intuitively speaking, this means the loss of
341   information is due to an implicit constant factor on SWpp (µd , ν d ), which we denote as the Effective
342   Subspace Scaling Factor (ESSF). Thus, rescaling the one-dimensional Wasserstein for all slices via
343   Equation (16) becomes multiplying the SWD by the reciprocal of the ESSF. We proceed further to
344   make this connection explicit by the following theorem.

372   4.4 I MPLICATIONS FOR LEARNING ALGORITHMS : I S SWD A LL YOU N EED ?
373   Assumption 4.1 naturally holds in common machine learning settings. In gradient-based learning,
374   data is typically processed in minibatches, leading to an effective bound on k related to batch size
375   B ≪ d. Additionally, real datasets often have feature (linear) correlations, potentially reducing k
376   further. Lastly, the ESSF
                            \ (L) factor, despite its variance, can be absorbed into the learning rate
377   during optimization. This reduces the problem to a single hyperparameter search for the optimal
      learning rate—a standard practice in machine learning workflows.

380   distributions lies in a subspace of dimension k ≤ min{2B, d}.

485   There exist various generative modeling setups with Sliced-Wasserstein (Kolouri et al., 2018; Desh-

      the latent space (d = 512) of an autoencoder (Pidhorskyi et al., 2020) pretrained on the 1024 × 1024

488
489   We evaluate SW variants on both unconditional generation and unpaired image-to-image translation
490   tasks. For generation, we follow Deshpande et al. (2018)’s SWG setup using a generator Gϕ (·) to
491   transform z ∈ R8 to latents X ∈ R512 . For translation, we modify this to use a residual generator
492
      transforming source domain X to target domain Y latents. Following Rombach et al. (2022), Korotin
      et al. (2023), we operate in an autoencoder’s latent space to sidestep the known dimensionality
493
      challenges of SWG (Deshpande et al. (2018),Nadjahi et al. (2021)). We train for 10000 iterations
494
      using vanilla gradient descent with batch size 2048. For translation, we evaluate on two FFHQ

522
523   6        C ONCLUSION
524
525
      In this paper, we revisit the classic Sliced-Wasserstein and rethink the current approaches that
      modify the slicing distribution to focus on informative slices. We introduce another perspective of
526
      rescaling the 1D Wasserstein distances based on slice ‘informativeness.’ By defining the notion
527
      of informativeness in terms of alignment with the data effective subspace, we show this rescaling
528   simplifies to a global scaling factor on the SW. This directly translates to the standard learning rate
529   search in gradient-based optimization (even with a finite number of slices). We then empirically show
530   that, in a wide variety of learning settings, a properly configured SW performs competitively with
531   other complex variants without the additional computation/memory overheads. This challenges the
532   notion that increasingly advanced methods are always necessary for improved performance. We show
533   that while standard SW may not be the universal solution for all scenarios, it remains to be a reliable
534   and efficient solution for common learning tasks.
535   Future research: Our work does not preclude further research to improve the Sliced-Wasserstein
536   using the current approaches, which have their own merits. In fact, the rescaling approach has deep
537   connections to modifying the slicing distributions. Nonetheless, our work provides a novel and
538   generalized perspective to interpret and address a major limitation of the classic SW. From this
539   angle, future research could investigate different choices for the rescaling function ρ under various
      assumptions about the data, as well as explore alternative notions of slice informativeness.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

      A     P ROOFS AND A DDITIONAL T HEORETICAL R ESULTS

      A.1   N OTATION
705
706          • Rd : d-dimensional Euclidean space, where d is a positive integer.
707          • Sd−1 := {x ∈ Rd : ∥x∥ = 1}: unit sphere defined in Rd .
708          • P(Rd ): set of all probability measures defined on Rd .
709
             • Pp (Rd ): set of probability measures whose p-th moment is finite, where p ≥ 1.
710
711          • Vk,d : set of all d × k orthogonal matrices, i.e.
712                                         Vk,d := {U ∈ Rd×k : U ⊤ U = Ik }.
713
714            Note, Sd−1 = V1,d .
715          • U = [U [:, 1], U [:, 2], . . . U [:, k]] ∈ Vk,d : an orthogonal matrix. For each i ∈ [1 : k],
716            U [:, i] ∈ Rd is the i-th column of U .
717            Note that U induces a linear function from Rd to Rk , i.e. x 7→ U ⊤ x. With abuse of notation,
718            we do not distinguish the matrix U and the corresponding linear mapping.
719
             • Span(U ): The linear subspace spanned by U , i.e.
720                                                                             ( k                     )
721                                                                              X
722                    Span(U ) := Span({U [:, 1], U [:, 2], . . . U [:, k]}) =     αi U [:, i] : αi ∈ R .
                                                                              i=1
723
724          • Vk ⊂ Rd : a k-dimensional subspace, where k is a positive integer with k ≤ d. Note, by
725            classical linear algebra theory, we have
726
727
                                                       Vk = Span(U )
728            for some U ∈ Vd,k . Note, given Vk , U is not uniquely determined.
729
             • Vk⊥ : perpendicular complement of Vk , which is a subspace of dimension d − k.
730
731          • µd , µ, ν d , ν ∈ P(Rd ): probability measures in d-dimensional space.
732          • Ld : Lebesgue measure in Rd .
733          • C0 (Rd ): set of all continuous functions defined on Rd which vanish at infinity.
734                     d
                    dµ                                                            d
735          • fµ = dL d : density of µ, that is, for all test functions ϕ ∈ C0 (R ):

736                                       Z                       Z

                                            Rd                   Rd
738
739          • X ∼ µ: A random variable/vector X following distribution µ. We say X is a realization of
740            µ.
741          • E[X] := E[µ], where X ∼ µ: expected value of X, i.e.
742                                                     Z

745          • mk (µ): k-th moment of measure µ. That is, given realization X ∼ µ, mk (µ) is defined by
746

748
             • V ar(X) := E[(X − E(X))⊤ (X − E(X))]: the covariance matrix of X (or the measure µ).
749
             • T# µ, where T : Rd → Rd is a function: push-forward measure µ under mapping T . That
750
751
               is, for all Borel sets A ⊂ Rd , we have
752                                               T# µ(A) = µ(T −1 (A)).
753
               Equivalently speaking, suppose X ∼ µ is a realization of µ, then T (X) ∼ T# µ.
754
755          • N (e, Σ): Gaussian distribution, where e ∈ Rd is the expected value, Σ ∈ Rd×d is the
               covariance matrix.

            • 0d : d × 1 vector where each entry is 0. Similarly, we define 1d .

            • Id : d × d identity matrix.
759         • U(Sd−1 ): Uniform distribution defined on Sd−1 .

761
            • θ, θd , θg : a d-dimensional vector.
762
763
            • θk : a k-dimensional vector.
764         • PVk := PU , where Vk = Span(U ): the projection mapping from Rd into subspace Vk , i.e.
765
                                       PVk (x) := PU (x) = U U ⊤ x, ∀x ∈ Rd .
766
767           Note, in this case: the mapping U : Rd → Rk with x 7→ U ⊤ x is the corresponding
768           parameterization function of projection PU .
769         • Γ(µ, ν): set of joint measures whose marginals are µ, ν respectively:
770
771                           Γ(µ, ν) := {γ ∈ P((Rd )2 ) : (π1 )# γ = µ, (π2 )# γ = ν},
772
              where π1 : (x, y) 7→ x, π2 : (x, y) 7→ y are canonical projection mappings.
773
774         • Wpp (µ, ν): Wasserstein problem between µ and ν:
775                                                         Z

                                                 γ∈Γ(µ,ν)      (Rd )2

780                                                   Z

782                                                       Sd−1

784           describes the information of the projected θ on the space spanned by U .

      Remark A.1. In this paper, we adopt the following convention.
797
798   We do not distinguish the scalar/vector/matrix and the corresponding induced linear mapping. For
799   example, θ ∈ Rd , induces the mapping
800                                         Rd ∋ x 7→ θ⊤ x ∈ R.
801
802         • When θ is a random vector, we refer to it as a “random projection mapping” in both the
803           main text and the appendix. We adopt the same convention for the scalar notation α and the
804           matrix notation U .
805
            • We use θ# µ to denote the push-forward measure induced by mapping x 7→ θ⊤ x. Similarly,
806
              (θ × θ)# γ denotes the push-forward measure of joint measure γ ∈ P((Rd )2 ) induced by
807
              mapping (x, x′ ) 7→ (θ⊤ x, θ⊤ x′ ). The same convention is adopted for α, U .
808
809   Remark A.2. For simplicity, in notation SW (µ, ν; σ), we may relax the restriction that σ is a
      probability measure. We allow σ to be a finite positive measure in the main text and appendix.

      A.2    WASSERSTEIN DISTANCES IN Rd AND Rk
811
812   In this article, we assume the probability measures µd , ν d ∈ Pp (Rd ) are supported in a lower
813   dimensional subspace. We refer to Assumption 4.1 for details.
814
      Let PU denote the projection mapping from Rd to Vk :
815
816                                         PU (x) = U U ⊤ x, ∀x ∈ Rd ,                               (23)
817   Then, the corresponding lower-dimensional parameterization mapping is defined as:
818
819                                                x 7→ U ⊤ x, ∀x ∈ Rd .                              (24)
820
821   By classical linear algebra theory, it is straightforward to verify the following:
822   Proposition A.3. [Basic properties of linear projection] Let PU , U be defined above, then we have:
823
            (1) For each θ ∈ Rd , θ can be uniquely decomposed into Vk , Vk⊥ , i.e. θ = θVk + θVk⊥ , where
824
825             θVk = PU (θ) ∈ Vk , θVk⊥ ∈ Vk⊥ .
826
            (2) For all x ∈ Vk , PU (x) = x.
827
828         (3) If we restrict U to space Vk , denoted as U |Vk , then U |Vk : V k → Rk is a bijection. The
829             inverse is given by
830                                              (U )−1 (y) = U y, ∀y ∈ Rk .
831
832             In addition, ∥U (x)∥ = ∥x∥, ∀x ∈ Vk .
833
834   Proof. It follows directly from the definitions of PU , U .
835
836   Let µk = (U )# µd , ν k = (U )# ν d , the above proposition directly induces the following relation
837   between the Wasserstein distance between µd , ν d and the Wasserstein distance between µk , ν k .
838   Proposition A.4. Under assumption 4.1, we have the following:
839
840
            (1) µd can be recovered by the inverse of U |Vk , i.e.
                                                                 ⊤ k
841                                                        µd = U# µ .
842
843         (2) The mapping
844
                                    Γ(µd , ν d ) ∋ γ d 7→ γ k := (U × U )# γ d ∈ Γ(µk , ν k ),        (25)
845
846
                is a well-defined bijection, where U × U is defined as
847
848                        Rd × Rd ∋ (x, x′ ) 7→ U × U ((x, x′ )) = (U (x), U (x′ )) ∈ Rk × Rk .      (26)
849
850         (3) The Wasserstein distance is preserved via the lower-dimensional parameterization:
851                              Wpp (µd , ν d ) = Wpp ((PU )# µd , (PU )# ν d ) = Wpp (µk , ν k )    (27)
852
853
      Proof. Let X ∼ µd be a realization.
854
855         (1) We have U ⊤ X ∼ µk since µk = (U )# µd . In addition, by assumption (4.1), we have
856

                                                          ⊤ k

859

861             Similarly, (π2 )# (U × U )# γ d = ν k . Thus the mapping defined in (25) is well-defined.
862             Moreover, from statement (1), we have
863

               Thus, (25) is invertible/bijection.

903   A.3    BACKGROUND : R ELATIONSHIP BETWEEN THE G AUSSIAN AND S PHERICAL U NIFORM
904
             D ISTRIBUTION
905
      In this section, we introduce basic properties of multivariate Gaussian and the relation between
906   Gaussian and spherical uniform distribution.
907
908   First we consider 1D space R, choose e ∈ R and σ > 0, the Gaussian distribution, denoted as
909
      N (e, σ 2 ), is the probability measure whose density is defined by
910                                                            1          (x−e)2

911
                                               f (x) := √            e− 2σ2 ,
                                                             2πσ 2
912
913   where e, σ 2 are the expected value and variance of X respectively.
914   When e = 0, σ 2 = 1, the induced measure is called standard (1D) Gaussian distribution, whose
915   density is given by
916
                                                             1  x2
917                                                f (x) := √ e− 2                                    (29)
                                                             2π


                                                              17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      In space Rd , the above density function can be generalized as:
919
920                                                       1       ∥x∥2

921
                                            f (x) :=        d/2
                                                                e− 2                                  (30)
                                                       (2π)
922
923   and the induced distribution is called d-dimensional Standard Gaussian distribution.
924
      Given e ∈ Rd and positive definite d × d matrix, Σ = AAT where A ∈ Rd×k , the Gaussian
925   distribution is denoted as N (e, Σ), can be defined by the following well-known proposition:
926
927   Proposition A.5 (Definition of Gaussian distribution). Let X ∼ N (e, Σ) be a realization, then the
928
      following are equivalent:
929
             • N (e, Σ) is Gaussian distribution, with expected value e and covariance matrix Σ.
930
931          • X = AG + e, where G ∼ N (0, Id ), whose density is defined by (30).
932
933          • ∀θ ∈ Rd , θ⊤ X is a 1D Gaussian variable:
934
935                                         θ⊤ X ∼ N (θ⊤ e, (θ⊤ A)⊤ (θ⊤ A)).
936
937   From the proposition, it is straightforward to verify the following:
938
      Proposition A.6 (Basic properties of Gaussian distribution). Suppose X ∼ N (e, Σ), then we have:
939
940
           (1) If rank(Σ) = d, then N (e, Σ) admits the density function:
941
942                                                      1           −
                                                                       (x−e)T Σ−1 (x−e)

943
                                       f (x) =                     e           2
                                                 (2π)d/2 det(Σ)1/2
944
945
946
           (2) Choose B ∈ Rd×k , β ∈ Rk , and let TB,e,β (x) := B(x − e) + β, then we have
947
                                 B(X − e) + β ∼ (TB,e,β )# N (e, Σ) = N (β, B ⊤ ΣB).
948
949
950        (3) Suppose Z ∼ N (0, Id ), then the absolute p-th power of Z is given by
951
952                                                                 Γ( p+d
                                                                        2 )
                                                 E[∥Z∥p ] = 2p/2            .
953                                                                 Γ(d/2)
954

       B.1       G RADIENT F LOW
1515
1516   B.1.1       BACKGROUND OVERVIEW
1517
1518   Let P(Rd ) denote the space of probability measures on Rd . For µ, ν ∈ P(Rd ), the gradient flow of
1519
       the SW distance in the space of probability measures evolves according to the continuity equation
1520
1521                                                        ∂µt
                                                                + ∇ · (vt µt ) = 0,                                                         (47)
1522                                                         ∂t
1523                                                                                                                        δSW 2 (µ ,ν)
                                                                                                t
1524   where µt is a time-dependent probability measure and vt the velocity field vt = −∇   2
                                                                                            δµt     . This
                                                                            d
1525   describes the transport of measure µt in the Wasserstein space P2 (R ), commonly referred to as the
1526   Wasserstein Gradient Flows (WGF, Ambrosio et al. (2008))
1527   For numerical simulation in practice, one discretizes this dynamic using a particle approximation.
1528   We let {xtt }N
                    i=1 denote a system of N particles evolving according to the following system of ODEs:
1529
1530                                                     dxti
1531                                                          = −∇xi SW22 (µN
                                                                            t , ν),                                                         (48)
                                                          dt
1532
                  1
                             PL
1533   where µN
              t = L
                                                                                                   t
                                i=1 δxti is the empirical measure based on the particle positions xi .
1534
1535
       These WGF particle-based approaches preserve key features of continuous systems and have been
       widely adopted, especially machine learning applications (Peyré et al. (2019).
1536

       B.3   D EEP G ENERATIVE M ODELING
1729
1730   In this section, we provide additional details on the model architecture and numerical results for the
1731   deep generative modeling tasks. We present both qualitative and quantitative results, including the
1732   W2 metric, to evaluate the model’s performance across different SW variants. The experiments were
1733   conducted using the FFHQ dataset.
1734   Model architecture:
1735
1736   For unconditional generation:
1737
1738
                    G(z) = FCd ◦ LeakyReLU0.2 ◦ BN ◦ FC1024

                               ◦ LeakyReLU0.2 ◦ BN ◦ FC512

1742
1743   For unpaired translation:
1744
1745

                        T (z) = z + FCd ◦ LeakyReLU0.2 ◦ BN ◦ FC1024
                                                                                                                (49)
1747                                       ◦ LeakyReLU0.2 ◦ BN ◦ FC1024 (z),           z ∈ R512

        Table 4: Quantitative comparison between different variants for Deep Generative Modeling 5.4.
